Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/medium/am-2019-07865b_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07865&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Composite cathodes have attracted great attention due to the integrated advantages of each pure structure. Also, the component ratio deserves a careful modulation to further improve the corresponding electrochemical performance. Mn-based layer–tunnel hybrid composite became a focus in sodium-ion batteries due to the superiority in terms of high performance, low cost, and nontoxicity. In the previous reports, the structure modulation was carried out via changing the synthesis condition, varying the transition-metal-element ratio, and different ion doping. Also, it is still challenging to explore a more feasible method to simplify the adjustment process. Herein, we introduced Mg<sup>2+</sup> into Na sites or transition-metal sites in Na<sub>0.6</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and first discovered the doping-site-variation-induced structural adjustment phenomenon. Specifically, Mg doping in transition-metal sites could be beneficial for the growth of the P2-type structure, while layer/tunnel component ratio decreased when locating Mg<sup>2+</sup> in Na sites. The P2–O2 phase transformations could be effectively suppressed by locating Mg<sup>2+</sup> in both sites in high-voltage regions and thus improve the cycling performance. The designed material, Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>Mg<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, could attain a decent capacity of 100 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and a satisfied retention of 76.6% after 500 cycles. Additionally, ex situ X-ray diffraction analysis experiments verify the excellent structural stability of Mg-substituted samples during charge–discharge processes. Moreover, the Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>Mg<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> also displays superior sodium-ion full-cell properties when merged with hard carbon anode. Thus, this research may indicate a proper novel thread for designing high-performance composite electrodes.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D26938%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJie%2BQu%252C%2BDong%2BWang%252C%2BZu-guang%2BYang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D30%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b07865%26title%3DIon-Doping-Site-Variation-Induced%2BComposite%2BCathode%2BAdjustment%253A%2BA%2BCase%2BStudy%2Bof%2BLayer%25E2%2580%2593Tunnel%2BNa0.6MnO2%2Bwith%2BMg2%252B%2BDoping%2Bat%2BNa%252FMn%2BSite%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D26945%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Chemical%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Chemical structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Physical%20and%20chemical%20processes" class="article__tags__link">Physical and chemical processes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transition%20metals" class="article__tags__link">Transition metals</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Mn-based+Cathode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Mn-based%20cathode%20materials">Mn-based cathode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Layer%E2%88%92tunnel+Hybrid+Composite&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_layer%E2%88%92tunnel%20hybrid%20composite">layer−tunnel hybrid composite</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Structure+Adjustment&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_structure%20adjustment">structure adjustment</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Mg+Doping&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Mg%20doping">Mg doping</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The rapid development of electrochemical energy storage devices has efficiently alleviated the energy crisis. Over the past two decades, advancements of lightweight lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have made it more accessible to store and restore electrical power wherever it is needed. However, limited availability and higher costs of lithium hinder the wide-scale implementation of the grid storage sectors. In this regard, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) stand as serious contenders with the unlimited supply of sodium and its even distribution around the world. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 1 to 16">(1−16)</a> Among all cathode materials, the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MnO<sub>2</sub> system has attracted much attention due to its environmental benignity and lower cost. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> Recently, appealing intergrowth structures with integration of synergistic advantages of various structures have posed excellent prospects for both LIBs and SIBs. Some pioneers have demonstrated that layered and spinel hybrid structures, P2–O3 and P3–P2 heterostructures, could exert profound superiority compared with the single-phase structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 19 to 22">(19−22)</a> In our past research studies, a layer–tunnel composite cathode Na<sub>0.6</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> was prepared via a simple co-precipitation, and its excellent electrochemical advantages could be attributed to the synergy effect of the composite material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Nevertheless, further advancement of the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MnO<sub>2</sub> system could be seriously hampered by its multiple phase transformations during charging and discharging processes. To circumvent these problems inherent to Mn-based oxides, an effective design strategy is needed. Fortunately, substituting Mn by other transition-metal (TM) elements is viewed as a useful method to stabilize the TMO<sub>2</sub> layer and thus enhance the electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 25 to 28">(25−28)</a> Furthermore, chemical substitution is also viewed as a reliable method to adjust structure components in heterostructure composites. However, most chemical substitutions are restricted to the replacement in TM sites. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 29 to 32">(29−32)</a> In this work, Mg<sup>2+</sup> was incorporated in Na-ion layers and TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, respectively, in the layer–tunnel hybrid Na<sub>0.6</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. We first discovered that different chemical substitution sites could result in different structural transformations in heterostructure composites. The layer/tunnel component ratio increased with locating Mg<sup>2+</sup> in TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, while Mg<sup>2+</sup> substitution in Na-ion layers could be beneficial to the growth of the tunnel structure. The existence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> in both sites could effectually suppress the transformations from P2 to O2 phases in high-voltage regions and thus improve the cycling performance. The excellent structural stability during charge–discharge processes was confirmed with the help of ex situ X-ray diffraction analysis (XRD) patterns. Also, the optimized sample displays excellent Na-ion full battery properties when coupled with hard carbon anode, thus indicating the application potential of the designed material.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Thus, this study realizes the modification of electrochemical performance and structural adjustment simultaneously in hybrid composite. Compared with traditional doping method, this paper aimed to study the impact of various chemical substitution sites on phase transformations of a layer–tunnel hybrid composite, which provides a fresh vision to design satisfactory cathode samples for SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, all of the materials (Na<sub>0.6</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.58</sub>Mg<sub>0.01</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.56</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>Mg<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.98</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, denoted as Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, Mg-0.02 A, Mg-0.01 T, and Mg-0.02 T, respectively) were prepared by an easy co-precipitation method with consequently high-temperature calcination. The stoichiometric ratios of sodium acetate (KESHI, 99%), manganese acetate (KESHI, 98%), and magnesium acetate (KESHI, 98%) were completely dissolved in 150 mL of deionized water. Aqueous solution with excess H<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O (KESHI, 98%) was added into the previous acetate solution as a precipitant. The mixture evaporated at 90 °C to form a precursor under continuous stirring at 40 °C for 180 min. The precursor complex was dried in vacuum oven at 120 °C overnight and then precalcined at 450 °C for 6 h with the final calcination at higher temperature (900 °C for 10 h). The heating and cooling rates were both maintained at 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. Eventually, the targeted samples were immediately moved and stored into a glovebox to filled with argon to avoid the water content in the air.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystalline structures of the synthesized samples were identified by powder X-ray diffraction analysis (XRD, Cu Kα radiation, PW 1730, Philips Company). Morphologies of the targeted samples were acquired by field emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM, HITACHI S-4800). The data of transmission electron microscopy (TEM) were explored on JEM-2100F. The detailed information of X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was determined on ESCALAB 250Xi. The specific ratios of various elements were obtained by inductively coupled plasma (ICP-OES, Thermo Electron IRIS Intrepid II XSP).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Electrodes were fabricated by fully blending the active cathode material (75 wt %), carbon black (15 wt %), and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (10 wt %) in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone as a dispersing agent. The slurry was then cast on aluminum foils and dried in a vacuum oven at 100 °C overnight. Electrochemical tests were carried out in CR2025 coin cells. The electrolyte solution consisted of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> (Aladdin, 99.99%). Pure Na metal was regarded as the counter electrode. All the half-cells were typically cycled within the voltage range of 2.0–4.0 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at a fixed temperature of 25 °C. A Zahner electrochemical station was used to carry out the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements, which were performed by a 5 mV amplitude between 100 kHz and 100 mHz.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The crystal phases of the prepared powders were identified by X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. All of the Bragg diffraction peaks of every sample could be assigned to the tunnel structure (space group: <i>Pbam</i>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 23 to 34">(23−34)</a> and P2 phase (space group: <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> without any other impurity peaks, indicating that Mg<sup>2+</sup> was successfully incorporated into the pristine layer–tunnel hybrid structures. It is worth mentioning that compared with Mg-0, stronger peaks assigned to a tunnel structure occurred in Mg-0.01 A and Mg-0.02 A, indicating that Mg-replacement in Na-ion sites could be beneficial to the growth of the tunnel structure. However, it shows an inverse trend when substituting minor Mn with Mg in TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs. With the greater Mg content, the tunnel structure peaks gradually disappeared, with more significant peaks indexed to the P2-type phase, which indicates that the substitution of Mg<sup>2+</sup> in TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs is beneficial to the growth of the layered structure. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a–c shows the Rietveld refinement profiles of Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, and Mg-0.01 T, respectively. A brilliant fitness for the calculated and observed patterns indicates suitable results based on the tunnel and layered phases. The details of structural parameters for Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, and Mg-0.01 T are summarized in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. Refined crystallographic sites and amount of Mg and Na in Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, and Mg-0.02 A samples are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>. The tunnel structure component in the hybrid composite increased apparently with Mg doping into Na<sup>+</sup> layers. To confirm that Mg<sup>2+</sup> is located in Na<sup>+</sup> layers, the shift trend of the (002) peak is analyzed. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, it is clear that the (002) peak shifts to a higher angle when Mg<sup>2+</sup> is located in the Na<sup>+</sup> layer (Mg-0.01 A). This peak is highly related to the transformation of the crystal lattice parameters. When substituting minor Na<sup>+</sup> with Mg<sup>2+</sup> in alkali metal slabs, the <i>c</i> parameter would decrease since the ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) is bigger than that of Mg<sup>2+</sup> (0.72 Å). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Thus, the <i>c</i> parameter would slightly decrease, corresponding to the higher angle of the (002) peak in Mg-0.01 A.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/medium/am-2019-07865b_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. XRD patterns of Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, Mg-0.02 A, Mg-0.01 T, and Mg- 0.02 T (a). XPS spectra of Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, and Mg-0.01 T for Mn 2p (b).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07865&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">However, this shift trend varies when the introduction minor Mg<sup>2+</sup> into TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Obviously, the (002) peak shifts to a lower angle with the Mg<sup>2+</sup> doping in TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, primarily because the ionic radius of Mg<sup>2+</sup> (0.72 Å) is larger than those of Mn<sup>3+</sup> (0.645 Å) and Mn<sup>4+</sup> (0.53 Å). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The different shift trend of the (002) peak could further confirm that Mg<sup>2+</sup> is successfully located in the Na<sup>+</sup> layer and TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs.</div><div class="NLM_p">To more directly identify whether Mg<sup>2+</sup> is located in the Na<sup>+</sup> layer, high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HADDF-STEM) images of Mg-0 and Mg-0.01 A were captured. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>, the bright contrast between two adjacent TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs is marked by green arrows in the magnified images. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Obviously, compared with Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A showed a brighter contrast, demonstrating that Mg<sup>2+</sup> successfully occupied the Na<sup>+</sup> layer.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/medium/am-2019-07865b_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. HADDF-STEM images of Mg-0 (a) and Mg-0.01 A (b).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07865&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the impacts of Mg<sup>2+</sup> substitution on the layer–tunnel composite material, obvious influences of Mg<sup>2+</sup> incorporation on the pure P2 phase, Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> coupled with the tunnel phase, Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, were also studied. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>, similar to Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.42</sub>Mg<sub>0.01</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> also indicates a certain tunnel-phase XRD pattern, demonstrating that Mg was successfully introduced into the Na-ion layer in Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. However, when substituting Mn with minor Mg in Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, an apparent peak around 15.5° assigned to the plane of 002 belonging to the P2 phase occurred. Additionally, another peak at 37.5° was also found, suggesting that Mg<sup>2+</sup> doping in TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs in the pure tunnel phase could cause the phase transformation from tunnel to P-type structure. As illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>, when incorporating a little Mg<sup>2+</sup> into the P2-type phase, Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, both Na<sub>0.65</sub>Mg<sub>0.01</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>Mg<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> show a pure P-type structure.</div><div class="NLM_p">The detailed compositions of the synthesized samples were analyzed by inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectroscopy and summarized in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>. The result is quite similar to the anticipated stoichiometry. The XPS tests were carried out to acquire the valence states of Mg and Mn ions of the prepared materials. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b shows the high-resolution XPS spectra of Mn 2p in Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, and Mg-0.01 T, respectively. Two major peaks attributed to Mn 2p<sup>1/2</sup> and Mn 2p<sup>3/2</sup> could be deconvoluted into four distinct peaks, indicating the coexistence of Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>, the ratios of Mn<sup>3+</sup> to Mn<sup>4+</sup> (denoted as Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>) between Mg-0 and Mg-0.01 A are 1.31 and 1.33, respectively. The negligible variation could be a result of Mg-replacement in the Na-ion layers, which may not influence the component of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> in TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> was successfully substituted with Mg<sup>2+</sup>. However, this ratio decreased apparently with the introduction of Mg<sup>2+</sup> in TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, which could be attributed to the lower valence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> compared with Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>. The decreased component of Mn<sup>3+</sup> also plays a key role in alleviating the Jahn–Teller effect resulting from the formation of high-spin Mn<sup>3+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> As illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1d</a>, the peaks of Mg 2p around 49.0 eV in Mg-0.01 A and Mg-0.01 T are assigned to existence of Mg<sup>2+</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">The morphologies of the as-prepared powders are observed by SEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a–c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>). Smooth surface facets could be found in all of the particles. Mg-0 is composed of sheetlike particles belonging to the P2 structure <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> and rodlike particles with a defined tunnel phase <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> (marked by green and blue circles, respectively), which clearly identifies the P2-tunnel hybrid structure. However, more rodlike morphologies could be found in the Mg-0.01 A (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b) sample, while the Mg-0.01 T sample shows an opposite trend (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c), corresponding to XRD refinement results discussed above. The respective mapping images of rodlike and sheetlike particles in Mg-0.01 T and Mg-0.01 A are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d–k,l–s. The Na and Mn elements are distributed evenly in all of the particles. For Mg-0.01 T, more Mg elements are distributed in the tunnel structure, which indicates that more Mg elements tend to be scattered on the layered phase in Mg-0.01 T.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/medium/am-2019-07865b_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. SEM images of Mg-0 (a), Mg-0.01 A (b), and Mg-0.01 T (c). Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy mapping results of Mg-0.01 T (d–k) and Mg-0.01 A (l–s).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07865&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To have more detailed insights about the influence of Mg substitution on the structural evolution, high-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) was carried out. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,d, at higher magnification, the microparticles further disclose a defined layer–tunnel composite structure, which is similar to SEM images. In the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b,c,e,f, HR-TEM images reveal a series of distinct lattice fringes, reflecting the excellent crystallinity of Mg-0 and Mg-0.0 T, respectively. For the Mg-0 sample, the HR-TEM image of rodlike particles displayed an even lattice fringe and the <i>d</i>-spacing of 4.67 Å (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b), which is in agreement with the plane of 200 in the tunnel phase. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c shows HR-TEM image of a platelike particle, indicating a distinct lattice fringe and <i>d</i>-spacing of 2.50 Å, which is in accordance with the plane of 100 in the P-type structure. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e,f, when introducing minor Mg into TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs into Mg-0, the <i>d</i>-spacings of rodlike and sheetlike particles increases to 4.72 and 2.53 Å, respectively, which could be attributed to the slightly smaller ionic radius of Mn<sup>3+</sup> (0.645 Å) or Mn<sup>4+</sup> (0.53 Å) than that of Mg<sup>2+</sup> (0.72 Å). Additionally, these desirable results also demonstrate that Mg<sup>2+</sup> incorporation into the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs allows larger room for Na-ion de/intercalation processes.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/medium/am-2019-07865b_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. TEM and high-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) images of Mg-0 (a–c) and Mg-0.01 T (d–f).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07865&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">These cycling performances of the as-prepared samples are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. In comparison to the pristine material, Mg-doped electrodes indicate better storage performances of Na<sup>+</sup>. The ultimate capacities were about 87 (Mg-0), 104 (Mg-0.01 A), 101 (Mg-0.02 A), 124 (Mg-0.01 T), and 102 (Mg-0.02 T) mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 circles at 1.0C, with capacity retentions of 67.5, 90.6, 91.6, 82.1, and 86.2%, respectively. It is clear that Mg-0.02 A shows the most significant improvement on the cycling performance because the Mg incorporation into Na-ion layers brings more tunnel structure components with superior structural stability during charge–discharge processes. However, with the doping of Mg in alkali metal layers, the specific capacity demonstrated an apparent decrease, which could be a result of the formation of more tunnel structures with low theoretical capacities. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> When substituting Mn with Mg in TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, both the cycling capability and cycling performance were boosted dramatically. Although the capacity retention (82.1%) slightly decreased compared with Mg-0.01 A, Mg reaches 151 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The enhancement in the capacity could be attributed to the introduction of P2-phase structure with a high theoretical capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Thus, Mg-0.01 T owns the best overall electrochemical performance compared with other samples. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b shows the rate performances of all five samples at different current densities between 0.2 and 8.0C for each five circles.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/medium/am-2019-07865b_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical performance of Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, Mg-0.02 A, Mg-0.01 T, and Mg-0.02 T cycled during the potential scope of 2.0–4.0 V (versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). The cycling performances (a) at 1C (1C rate corresponding 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and rate capabilities (b) from 0.2 to 8.0C of the as-prepared samples. Certain charge–discharge plots (c) of Mg-0.01 T//Na and hard carbon//Na in a half-cell configuration and cycling performance (d) of SIBs based on Mg-0.01 T//hard carbon configuration at 0.1C. Long cycling property of Mg-0.01 T at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (e).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07865&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Also, Mg-0.01 T displays a better rate capability compared with other samples. Even at 1600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, it also presents a decent specific capacity (88 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). In addition, the Mg-0.01 T electrode delivers an excellent capacity retention of 76.6% after 500 cycles, with a surprisingly stable Coulombic efficiency even at a high rate of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e). By comparison, Mg-0 exhibits an extremely rapid capacity drop with severe fluctuation of Coulombic efficiency (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">Apart from boosting electrochemical performances in half-full cell, it also dramatically shows brilliant full-cell property to preliminarily indicate the practicability of the Mg-0.01 T cathode material. The charging/discharging plots of the Mg-0.01 T cathode and the hard carbon anode were tested in the half battery coupled with metallic sodium foil as the counter electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d, the full battery presents a decent first discharge capacity (100 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) and the average Coulombic efficiency is above 99% almost during the whole cycling process. Additionally, after 50 cycles at 0.2C, the capacity retention is 78% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). These desirable performances make this material a promising candidate for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">To study the evolution of the electrochemical behavior of the as-prepared electrodes, the d<i>q</i>/d<i>v</i> and galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles have been performed in sodium half-cells. The first charge–discharge curves of Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, and Mg-0.01 T are illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. It is clear that the charge capacity of the as-prepared samples is less than the discharge capacity, which could be a result of the insufficient initial sodium content of the as-prepared materials. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a shows the second d<i>q</i>/d<i>v</i> curves, where various pairs of redox peaks could be observed, which could be attributed to the coexistence of the P-type and tunnel phases. The prominent redox peaks between 2.0 and 2.5 V in Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, and Mg-0.01 T may be assigned to Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> in the P2 layered structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Apart from Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>, there are other ways to provide charge capacity. Take the second charge profile of Mg-0.01 T as an example, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, the long charge plateau around 2.4 V is attributed to Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>, while the platform above 3.5 V is associated with the release of O<sub>2</sub>. The smoother curve between 2.5 and 3.5 V indicates an evident solid–solution reaction mechanism. At the same time, smoother curves also evidence the change of the ordered structures during charging/discharging processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Apparently, as shown by blue rectangle in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a, multiple redox couples representing charging/discharging processes of the tunnel phase occur in Mg-0 and Mg-0.01 A, which demonstrates complicated transformation procedures to alleviate the structural strain because of the de/intercalation processes of Na ion. However, it almost disappeared in Mg-0.01 T, since the tunnel phase component in the layer–tunnel composite structure decreased obviously due to the introduction of Mg in TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, corresponding to the smoother GCD curve of Mg-0.01 T in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b. As shown by red rectangle in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a, the irreversible oxidation peaks above 3.5 V may be attributed to the P2–O2 structural transformation, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> which is accompanied by the peeling and expansion of TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs where Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalate. Surprisingly, such a detrimental phase transition nearly fades away in Mg-0.01 A and Mg-0.01 T, which could account for the dramatic enhancement of the cycling performance.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/medium/am-2019-07865b_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) d<i>q</i>/d<i>v</i> and (b) galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles during the second cycle of Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, and Mg-0.01 T electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07865&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">These possible structural transitions when sodium ions are (de)inserted were investigated by ex situ XRD tests. Apart from the H<sub>2</sub>O and Al substances at around 13.5, 25.5 and 65.5°, respectively. The remaining peaks can be assigned to tunnel/layered phases. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a> For Mg-0.01 A sample, all of the major reflections associated with the P2 phase and tunnel structure were well-retained, indicating the excellent structural stability during various charge/discharge states (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a). Moreover, the amplified parts of Mg-0.01 A from 15 to 17° are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b,c. The magnified (002) peak nearly remains unchanged in different charge/discharge states. The brilliant reversibility could have originated from the more tunnel phase formed in the layer–tunnel hybrid structure when minor Mg is introduced into the Na<sup>+</sup> layer; as a result, it could alleviate the structural transitions because of the superior stability of the tunnel phase. Also, the excellent phase stability of Mg-0.01 A is consistent with its impressive cycling performance in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a. For Mg-0.01 T, as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d, the 002 peak from 15 to 17° slightly shifts towards a smaller angle when charged to 4.0 V as a result of the gradual expansion of the interlayer space due to Na<sup>+</sup> removal from the lattice during charging process; as a result, it would consequently intensify electrostatic repulsions between adjacent O atoms. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> However, when it was discharged to 2.0 V, the 002 peak nearly came back to the original state (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>e,f), indicating a high structural reversibility. As expected, during the subsequent discharging process, with Na<sup>+</sup> returning to the main structure, the (002) peaks can gradually return to the original state, which is similar to the other reported results. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a></div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/medium/am-2019-07865b_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Ex situ XRD patterns of Mg-0.01 A (a–c) and Mg-0.01 T (d–f) at various charge/discharge voltages.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07865&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">EIS measurements, which were performed in Na half batteries after 100 circles at 200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, were carried out to study the influence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> doping on the charge-transfer resistance and Na-diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) of Mg-substituted samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a,b). The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> was obtained by <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">eqs S1 and S2</a>. Apparently, the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of Mg-substituted samples is much smaller than that of the Mg-0 sample. Additionally, as displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>, the <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A,</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/medium/am-2019-07865b_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. EIS spectra of Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A and Mg-0.01 T (a). The relationship between <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub> and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> in the low frequency region (b).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/asset/images/large/am-2019-07865b_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b07865&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">and Mg-0.01 T samples are about 0.13 × 10<sup>–13</sup>, 0.26 × 10<sup>–13</sup>, and 0.16 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Obviously, Mg-0.01 A presented the fastest sodium-ion diffusion.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, Mg<sup>2+</sup> ions have been successfully incorporated into the Na-ion layer and TMO2 slabs in a layer–tunnel hybrid composite Na<sub>0.6</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode. We first discovered that various doping sites in Na<sub>0.6</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> could bring different structural transformations. The layer/tunnel component ratio apparently decreased by locating Mg<sup>2+</sup> in Na-ion layers, while Mg<sup>2+</sup> substitution in TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs could be beneficial to the growth of the tunnel phase. In contrast to pristine sample, Mg-substituted materials all display a better Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance. Mg-0.01 A displayed an amazing cycling retention of 91.6% after 100 cycles at 1C, while Mg-0.01 T represented an initial discharge capacity of 100 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5C and obtained an excellent capacity retention of 76.6% after 500 cycles. Such a dramatic enhancement could be the result of different transformations and an effective inhibition of P2–O2 phase transitions during Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation processes. Additionally, GITT and CV technologies were implemented to testify the ameliorative Na-mobility kinetics. Moreover, the brilliant full battery performance coupled with hard carbon could ulteriorly indicate the promising application potential. This research provides a novel modification thread compared with traditional doping strategy and structural adjustment in heterostructure composites. We believe that this structure optimization method could be easily applied to other hybrid composites employed in future practical SIBs for wider application.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b07865" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b07865</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Rietveld refinement of the as-synthesized Mg-0, Mg-0.01 A, and Mg-0.01 T and the high-resolution XPS spectra of Mg 2p in Mg-0.01 A and Mg-0.01 T; X-ray diffraction patterns of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.42</sub>Mg<sub>0.01</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>Mn<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.65</sub>Mg<sub>0.01</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>Mg<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub>; SEM images of Mg-0.02 A and Mg-0.02 T; extended cyclic performance of Mg-0 at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; GITT curves of Mg-0.01 A for the second charge process between 2.0 and 4.0 V (current density: 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup> corresponding to 0.05C, time interval: 5 h); <i>t</i> versus <i>E</i> profile of Mg-0.01 A for a single-charged and -discharged GITT titration (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b07865/suppl_file/am9b07865_si_001.pdf">am9b07865_si_001.pdf (633.72 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b07865" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A new class of Solvent-in-Salt electrolyte for high-energy rechargeable metallic lithium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Suo Liumin; Hu Yong-Sheng; Li Hong; Armand Michel; Chen Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Liquid electrolyte plays a key role in commercial lithium-ion batteries to allow conduction of lithium-ion between cathode and anode.  Traditionally, taking into account the ionic conductivity, viscosity and dissolubility of lithium salt, the salt concentration in liquid electrolytes is typically less than 1.2 mol l(-1).  Here we show a new class of 'Solvent-in-Salt' electrolyte with ultrahigh salt concentration and high lithium-ion transference number (0.73), in which salt holds a dominant position in the lithium-ion transport system.  It remarkably enhances cyclic and safety performance of next-generation high-energy rechargeable lithium batteries via an effective suppression of lithium dendrite growth and shape change in the metallic lithium anode.  Moreover, when used in lithium-sulphur battery, the advantage of this electrolyte is further demonstrated that lithium polysulphide dissolution is inhibited, thus overcoming one of today's most challenging technological hurdles, the 'polysulphide shuttle phenomenon'.  Consequently, a coulombic efficiency nearing 100% and long cycling stability are achieved.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRIZQvO3WT9PDslzEEsg76ufW6udTcc2eZkilXiQWox7Lntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC3sznslKgtA%253D%253D&amp;md5=cb719ace996906c08eadedf61644e3dc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms7929"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shan, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na<sup>+</sup> Intercalation Pseudocapacitance in Graphene-Coupled Titanium Oxide Enabling Ultra-fast Sodium Storage and Long-term Cycling</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na+ intercalation pseudocapacitance in graphene-coupled titanium oxide enabling ultra-fast sodium storage and long-term cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Chaoji; Wen, Yanwei; Hu, Xianluo; Ji, Xiulei; Yan, Mengyu; Mai, Liqiang; Hu, Pei; Shan, Bin; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are emerging as a highly promising technol. for large-scale energy storage applications.  However, it remains a significant challenge to develop an anode with superior long-term cycling stability and high-rate capability.  Here we demonstrate that the Na+ intercalation pseudocapacitance in TiO2/graphene nanocomposites enables high-rate capability and long cycle life in a sodium-ion battery.  This hybrid electrode exhibits a specific capacity of above 90 mA h g-1 at 12,000 mA g-1 (∼36 C).  The capacity is highly reversible for more than 4,000 cycles, the longest demonstrated cyclability to date.  First-principle calcns. demonstrate that the intimate integration of graphene with TiO2 reduces the diffusion energy barrier, thus enhancing the Na+ intercalation pseudocapacitive process.  The Na-ion intercalation pseudocapacitance enabled by tailor-deigned nanostructures represents a promising strategy for developing electrode materials with high power d. and long cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrGrV7K8Tq5mLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhZ5leEEgzkCA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtF2lurzK&amp;md5=99303fd99ef59044874690750282067a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1021/cm5011696"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abakumov, A.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tsirlin, A.-A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bakaimi, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Van Tendeloo, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lappas, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Multiple Twinning As a Structure Directing Mechanism in Layered Rock-Salt-Type Oxides: NaMnO<sub>2</sub> Polymorphism, Redox Potentials, and Magnetism</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Multiple Twinning as a Structure Directing Mechanism in Layered Rock-Salt-Type Oxides: NaMnO2 Polymorphism, Redox Potentials, and Magnetism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Abakumov, Artem M.; Tsirlin, Alexander A.; Bakaimi, Ioanna; Van Tendeloo, Gustaaf; Lappas, Alexandros</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3306-3315</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">New polymorphs of NaMnO2 were obsd. using TEM and synchrotron x-ray powder diffraction.  Coherent twin planes confined to the (NaMnO2) layers, parallel to the (10‾1) crystallog. planes of the monoclinic layered rock-salt-type α-NaMnO2 (O3) structure, form quasi-periodic modulated sequences, with the known α- and β-NaMnO2 polymorphs as the two limiting cases.  The energy difference between the polymorphic forms, estd. using a DFT-based structure relaxation, is on the scale of the typical thermal energies that results in a high degree of stacking disorder in these compds.  The results unveil the remarkable effect of the twin planes on both the magnetic and electrochem. properties.  The polymorphism drives the magnetic ground state from a quasi-1D spin system for the geometrically frustrated α-polymorph through a two-leg spin ladder for the intermediate stacking sequence toward a quasi-2D magnet for the β-polymorph.  A substantial increase of the equil. potential for Na deintercalation upon increasing the concn. of the twin planes is calcd., providing a possibility to tune the electrochem. potential of the layered rock-salt ABO2 cathodes by engineering the materials with a controlled concn. of twins.  Crystallog. data and at. coordinates are given.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpmmKH46Btey7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li0QREgD8yfZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXnt1amtbk%253D&amp;md5=6922eb27042c67f89f49c92df71e9385</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03361B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishida, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-performance Symmetric Sodium-Ion Batteries Using a New, Bipolar O3-type Material, Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub></span>. <i>Energy Environ.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-performance symmetric sodium-ion batteries using a new, bipolar O3-type material, Na0.8Ni0.4Ti0.6O2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Shaohua; Yu, Haijun; Liu, Pan; Ren, Yang; Zhang, Tao; Chen, Mingwei; Ishida, Masayoshi; Zhou, Haoshen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Based on low-cost and rich resources, sodium-ion batteries have been regarded as a promising candidate for next-generation energy storage batteries in the large-scale energy applications of renewable energy and smart grids.  However, there are some crit. drawbacks limiting its application, such as safety and stability problems.  In this work, a stable sym. sodium-ion battery based on the bipolar, active O3-type material, Na0.8Ni0.4Ti0.6O2, is developed.  This bipolar material shows a typical O3-type layered structure, contg. two electrochem. active transition metals with redox couples of Ni4+/Ni2+ and Ti4+/Ti3+, resp.  This Na0.8Ni0.4Ti0.6O2-based sym. cell exhibits a high av. voltage of 2.8 V, a reversible discharge capacity of 85 mA h g-1, 75% capacity retention after 150 cycles and good rate capability.  This full sym. cell will greatly contribute to the development of room-temp. sodium-ion batteries with a view towards safety, low cost and long life, and it will stimulate further research on sym. cells using the same active materials as both cathode and anode.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqmF3B7AwSKZ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li0QREgD8yfZQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXitlamsLw%253D&amp;md5=ea808bbce1fe8dbd563aeb8d25a9e1e7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1038/nmat4551"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grimaud, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, W.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, S.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Anionic Redox Processes for Electrochemical Devices</span>. <i>Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">As a promising material for sodium-ion batteries, molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) affords excellent electrochem. performance owing to its large surface area and the accelerated electron transport within individual layers.  However, it suffers from slow reaction kinetics and agglomeration owing to low cond. and high surface energy.  In this work, nitrogen-doped carbon nanofiber@MoS2 nanosheets arrays with S-vacancies (NC@MoS2-VS) are developed via a process involving electrospining, hydrothermal and annealing.  When served as an anode material for SIBs, this material displays a superior capacity of 495 mAh g-1 over 100 charge/discharge cycles at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1, and the pseudocapacitive contribution is up to 74.4% as revealed by the cyclic voltammogram (CV) at 1 mV s-1.  The theor. calcns. show that the presence of sulfur vacancies facilitates the adsorption of Na+ and enhances the cond. of MoS2.  This work may pave a new avenue to develop other types of metal sulfides for high-performance SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGratUmpFgtRubVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgK53U_1a1cNQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhsVyntbjI&amp;md5=c7d10c509a8fecd99ee3403693b81698</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1016/j.jechem.2018.09.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.-D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Z.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, J.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ni, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Molybdenum and tungsten chalcogenides for lithium/sodium-ion batteries: Beyond MoS<sub>2</sub></span>. <i>J.
